<div id="review_3515857695" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3515857695" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7467113-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600867751p2/7467113.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3515857695?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/7467113-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7467113-mark?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7467113-mark?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3515857695" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10192131879862647213"><b>
  <i> “No more terrible disaster could befall your people than for them to fall into the hands of a Hero.” </i>
</b><br/><br/>No, that’s not a quote from <i>this</i> book, rather it is one of Liet-Kynes’ thoughts from the original <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44767458.Dune__Dune___1_' title='Dune (Dune, #1) by Frank Herbert' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dune</a>... but oh my, what a piece of foreshadowing that was! The quote works quite well as a tagline for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44492285.Dune_Messiah__Dune_Chronicles___2_' title='Dune Messiah (Dune Chronicles, #2) by Frank Herbert' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dune Messiah</a>. <br/><br/><i>Dune Messiah</i> is a very different book from <i>Dune</i>, and yet it’s impossible to discuss it without the context of the first novel. That’s because <i>Messiah</i>, despite taking place twelve </span>
  <span id="freeText10192131879862647213" style="display:none"><b>
  <i> “No more terrible disaster could befall your people than for them to fall into the hands of a Hero.” </i>
</b><br /><br />No, that’s not a quote from <i>this</i> book, rather it is one of Liet-Kynes’ thoughts from the original <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44767458.Dune__Dune___1_" title="Dune (Dune, #1) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune</a>... but oh my, what a piece of foreshadowing that was! The quote works quite well as a tagline for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/44492285.Dune_Messiah__Dune_Chronicles___2_" title="Dune Messiah (Dune Chronicles, #2) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune Messiah</a>. <br /><br /><i>Dune Messiah</i> is a very different book from <i>Dune</i>, and yet it’s impossible to discuss it without the context of the first novel. That’s because <i>Messiah</i>, despite taking place twelve years later, feels less like a self-contained story in its own right (although it does have a central plot which is resolved therein), and more like an extended aftermath to that initial volume. In it, we learn about the consequences of Paul’s status as a godlike ruler. And in all his flaws and moments of regret, Paul felt more real, more <i>human</i> in this novel than he ever did in <i>Dune</i>. <br /><br />While the story was interesting enough with its scheming and intrigue, and the added worldbuilding elements were fun, the book's true strength lies with its core message. In no uncertain terms, Frank Herbert was warning about how dangerous it can be to blindly and unquestioningly follow our leaders. <br /><br />Although this book doesn’t capture the grand majesty of <i>Dune</i>, I appreciate that it’s not a rehash of the first novel. Instead of a sprawling epic, this is a more intimate, character-driven book. That isn't necessarily a bad thing, but I can see why some readers might have been disappointed upon its initial publication.<br /><br />My biggest complaint is that my favorite character from the first book (Jessica) is essentially absent from this one. <br /><br />Overall, it’s a thought-provoking novel, it just didn’t engage me as much as I’d hoped.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10192131879862647213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1763817879" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1763817879" />
    <a title="Mike&#39;s Book Reviews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59173659-mike-s-book-reviews"><img alt="Mike&#39;s Book Reviews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599347737p2/59173659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1763817879?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike&#39;s Book Reviews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike&#39;s Book Reviews" href="/user/show/59173659-mike-s-book-reviews">Mike&#39;s Book Reviews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780425074985" href="/book/show/1829194.Dune_Messiah">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1763817879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5626786514012694999">It's difficult for me to be too difficult on this book because it simply feels incomplete. About half the size of the original, it feels like a simply bridge to Children of Dune than an actual sequel to Dune. But the theme of the story is one that Frank Herbert must have believed personally and that is Think For Yourself. People are not gods. Gods are not governance. To deify politicians and world leaders is a can of worms that should never be opened.<br/><br/>Fans of the original fell in love with Paul A</span>
  <span id="freeText5626786514012694999" style="display:none">It's difficult for me to be too difficult on this book because it simply feels incomplete. About half the size of the original, it feels like a simply bridge to Children of Dune than an actual sequel to Dune. But the theme of the story is one that Frank Herbert must have believed personally and that is Think For Yourself. People are not gods. Gods are not governance. To deify politicians and world leaders is a can of worms that should never be opened.<br /><br />Fans of the original fell in love with Paul Atreides and to see him essentially as a flawed leader with clay feet left a bitter taste in many mouths. So, again, it's easy to understand the criticism. It's like if Empire Strikes Back came out and Luke turned out to be just some dude who was more lucky than gifted. I’ll always recommend reading Messiah and Childreb of Dune together. Because as a stand alone, Messiah vanishes under the vast shadow cast by its predecessor.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5626786514012694999" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_691542126" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/691542126" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/691542126?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780441172696" href="/book/show/106.Dune_Messiah">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer691542126" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14835742434065221344">I don't normally look at reviews of a book prior to writing my own take on it, but sometime I just draw a blank after finishing a book. Some books are harder to review than others, sometime because I feel ambivalent about them, sometime I don’t fully understand them, and sometime I don’t know the reason, they just are. After finishing <i>Dune Messiah</i> I feel like I need some kind of launching pad to start off the review, some inspiration or perhaps I will resort to simply ripping off somebody’s revi</span>
  <span id="freeText14835742434065221344" style="display:none">I don't normally look at reviews of a book prior to writing my own take on it, but sometime I just draw a blank after finishing a book. Some books are harder to review than others, sometime because I feel ambivalent about them, sometime I don’t fully understand them, and sometime I don’t know the reason, they just are. After finishing <i>Dune Messiah</i> I feel like I need some kind of launching pad to start off the review, some inspiration or perhaps I will resort to simply ripping off somebody’s review wholesale (unfortunately <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=favourites" rel="nofollow noopener">Cecily</a> has not reviewed this one yet so I'll pass on the last option ;)<br /> <br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune__Dune_Chronicles___1_" title="Dune (Dune Chronicles, #1) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune</a>, as you are undoubtedly aware, is probably the most famous sci-fi novel of all time. <i>Dune Messiah</i> is like Frank Herbert’s equivalent of Michael Jackson’s “Bad” album in that it has to follow up a once in a lifetime mega hit and is doomed to come up short. Having read the book I do not get the feeling that Frank Herbert was feeling under pressure to match Dune’s success. Perhaps authors are not subject to the same level of pressure as pop stars. <br /><br />At around 340 pages <i>Dune Messiah</i> is about half the length of <i>Dune</i>, it is also very different in tone and pacing. It starts off twelve years after the events of <i>Dune</i>. Our literally know it all hero Paul "Muad'Dib" Atreides is now Emperor of the known universe and is having a suitably heroic melancholic time of it on account of the jihad which caused billions of death in his name. In the meantime powerful enemies are ganging up to snuff him out because he is too powerful, he is literally a know-it-all thanks to his oracular powers, and nobody likes a smartass. His <s>wife</s> concubine can not have a baby because his legal wife slipped her some contraceptive (and oracular powers apparently do not cover food additives). To make matters worse (or perhaps better) his dead teacher Duncan Idaho is returned to him as a sort of clone (ghola) with a suspicious mission and a new highly ominous name of Hayt. With all the odds stacked against him how can he survive? With panache of course!<br /><br />The first third of the book is very interesting with all the aforementioned odds being piled up against Paul, then the pacing of the book begin to sag with a lot of ruminations and philosophizing by the major characters and my mind drifted off to parts unknown. After a rather dry 100 or so pages the plot revives quite a bit and the climax is quite thrilling (if not exactly unpredictable). <br /><br />This book clearly has a lot of depth, themes and subtexts, unfortunately its profundity mostly escaped me as profundities tend to do. One of the Amazon reviewers mentioned that the book is so profound wh8ile reading it he frequently had to stop to think about what Herbert was <i>really</i> saying. The stoppages I made are mostly to do with thinking about my options for lunch and other mundane things.<br /><br />The two central characters are less compelling than they were in the previous book, Paul is all broody and miserable, his sister Alia goes through mood swings between being supernaturally sage, overly shrill and a teenager with a crush. Hayt/Idaho is pretty cool though, is he or isn’t he? Of course he is!<br /> <br />For me <i>Dune Messiah</i> acts as a slightly dull (but not too shabby) bridge to go on to the original trilogy’s grand finale <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/112.Children_of_Dune__Dune_Chronicles___3_" title="Children of Dune (Dune Chronicles, #3) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Children of Dune</a> which is brilliant by all accounts and I am looking forward to reading soonish.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14835742434065221344" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer17580499380112538274">Never has my fickle reader's heart been as frustrated and wrenched as it was while reading Dune Messiah. I must have put it down and swore not to pick it up again at least three or four times, but if you know anything about Dune, that's a declaration you can't follow through on. The Dune Chronicles just keeps getting better and better, this was probably Paul's greatest test, and damn, what a prolific writer Frank Herbert is, telling us the reader exactly what evil is being planned against the At</span>
  <span id="freeText17580499380112538274" style="display:none">Never has my fickle reader's heart been as frustrated and wrenched as it was while reading Dune Messiah. I must have put it down and swore not to pick it up again at least three or four times, but if you know anything about Dune, that's a declaration you can't follow through on. The Dune Chronicles just keeps getting better and better, this was probably Paul's greatest test, and damn, what a prolific writer Frank Herbert is, telling us the reader exactly what evil is being planned against the Atriedes and letting us hope and trust Paul's prescience to get him through it all. What a Bravo ending after such a trying tour de force of a build up. It's sad what happens to Channi and to Paul himself, but the sadness doesn't take away from the masterpiece that's the rest of the novel. It's written so well you almost accept it as a kindness. <br />This is a book, like Paul Atriedes himself, that I won't forget, not for a long time. A masterpiece.</span>

<div id="review_3634855471" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3634855471" />
    <a title="Nadine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3759963-nadine"><img alt="Nadine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566849081p2/3759963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3634855471?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nadine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nadine" href="/user/show/3759963-nadine">Nadine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3634855471" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8683354798693696294">Dune Messiah is an incredibly different novel than its predecessor, Dune. Taking place twelve years after the events of Dune, Dune Messiah digs into the nitty gritty of Paul’s rule as emperor by taking a philosophical approach to it. <br/><br/>As with Dune, Herbert throws his readers into the deep end with no floatation device. Dune Messiah is a difficult book to situate yourself in, especially if you haven’t read the previous novel in awhile. New characters, new cultures, and new technologies are introdu</span>
  <span id="freeText8683354798693696294" style="display:none">Dune Messiah is an incredibly different novel than its predecessor, Dune. Taking place twelve years after the events of Dune, Dune Messiah digs into the nitty gritty of Paul’s rule as emperor by taking a philosophical approach to it. <br /><br />As with Dune, Herbert throws his readers into the deep end with no floatation device. Dune Messiah is a difficult book to situate yourself in, especially if you haven’t read the previous novel in awhile. New characters, new cultures, and new technologies are introduced with little to no explanation. For the first half of the novel I felt like I was drowning in this world.<br /><br />Dune Messiah is a philosophical novel. I took a couple of philosophy courses in university and hated them. So, Dune Messiah was a difficult read for me to get through. Twelve years later, Paul is more than just an emperor, he’s seen as a god. After reading countless reviews for Dune Messiah and interviews with the author about the novel, Herbert said that he wanted readers to see Paul as a warning of the dangers of seeing powerful people as godlike. This is a central tenet to the novel hence the many heavily influenced philosophical conversations. Where Dune is full of action, Dune Messiah is slow as Herbert takes considerable time trying to get his point across.<br /><br />What I enjoyed most about Dune Messiah is the heavy foreshadowing. Maybe foreshadowing isn’t the right word since Paul’s ultimate fate is told to readers directly within the first few pages. Readers get to experience Paul’s downfall firsthand and witness the consequences of his actions.<br /><br />Overall, Dune Messiah is a difficult read if you’re not a fan of heavy philosophical debate. The ending of the novel sets up what is to come in the future making me cautiously optimistic.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8683354798693696294" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a title="Andreas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andreas" href="/user/show/20379307-andreas">Andreas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780441172696" href="/book/show/106.Dune_Messiah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20379307-andreas?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20379307-andreas?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20379307-andreas?shelf=classic-sff">classic-sff</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer747783727" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4121917841494815380"><cite>
  <em>“Truth suffers from too much analysis. -Ancient Fremen Saying”</em>
</cite><br/><br/>This <a href='https://reiszwolf.wordpress.com/2020/07/11/dune-•-1965-•-sf-novel-by-frank-herbert/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dune </a>successor tells the other half of Paul Muad'Dib's story who rose from underdog to a very human god, struggling with the devils that came with him. Intrigues and hidden meanings lurk around every single corner and complement Arrakis' beautiful extremes.<br/><br/>Mysticism, religion, holy war, politics and Tleilaxu biotech and shapeshifters complement the character development of Paul and his sister Alia - from heroes to gods and ba</span>
  <span id="freeText4121917841494815380" style="display:none"><cite>
  <em>“Truth suffers from too much analysis. -Ancient Fremen Saying”</em>
</cite><br /><br />This <a href="https://reiszwolf.wordpress.com/2020/07/11/dune-•-1965-•-sf-novel-by-frank-herbert/" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune </a>successor tells the other half of Paul Muad'Dib's story who rose from underdog to a very human god, struggling with the devils that came with him. Intrigues and hidden meanings lurk around every single corner and complement Arrakis' beautiful extremes.<br /><br />Mysticism, religion, holy war, politics and Tleilaxu biotech and shapeshifters complement the character development of Paul and his sister Alia - from heroes to gods and back again to normal humans. Paul tries to stop the juggernaut but he has to put everything in it.<br /><br />I've read this book a couple of times, though not as often as Dune (my very personal review is <a href="https://reiszwolf.wordpress.com/2020/07/11/dune-•-1965-•-sf-novel-by-frank-herbert/" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>). I always wondered about its size, and if it could be understood as just another fourth part of Dune, changing the original tension arc to a very different one, a deconstruction of Paul ending in a Greek tragedy. This time, I read the book very differently: not in one rush but slowly, a chapter a day as a buddy read.<br /><br /><i>“Now I am free.”</i><br /><br />One of the most interesting characters is the Tleilaxu ghola Hayt, enriching the world building with the new dimension of biotech. He slowly gets back the memories of Duncan Idaho. But he is not only just so transforming back, but a worthwhile individual with a conflicting combination of Mentat with Zensunni background. His discussion of consciousness and the implications on eternal life versus Paul's precognition in space and time was fascinating and gave me a lot to think.<br /><br />With my current re-read, I discovered the focus on female characters: First of all the huge hole of absence of Lady Jessica, who was mentioned only a couple of times and lives her life on Caladan. Paul's sister Alia of the Knifes gets a central virgin-harlot role and we see her struggle with her teenage hormones - with all her awakened experience, the human body takes its early toll, and a romance evolves:<br /><br />﻿<i>“This one must have a mate!” Stilgar blurted. “There’ll be trouble if she’s not wed, and that soon.”</i><br /><br />Chani is still the tough woman supporting Paul, and she gets one of the most memorable sentences when Paul asked her to refrain from her Fremen harshness and she accepted this: <br /><i>She did it the way sand accepted water: absorbing and concealing.</i><br /><br />Gaius Helen Mohiam plays her role as tough negotiator and I still remember the walk of the old Reverend Mother through Paul's oversized reception hall.<br /><br />The logic of the book has weaknesses but it has got the same epic, pompous grandeur and literary style as its predecessor. Plus it is more concentrated and brisker. <br /><br />I was really missing the story of the 12 years following Dune. But Frank Herbert skipped that time and we have to read about those by his son in Paul of Dune. It is a deconstruction of the first book's hero, leading to a Greek tragedy which has to end in the hero's death, freeing him from Jihad and deification. "“There will be a worm for Muad’dib,” they said." <br /><br />In general, this novel is more character driven than story driven. It seriously lacks action but compensates in dialogues. With an intrigue led by the Tleilaxu together with Bene Genesserit and the Guild, the book follows a simple plot and builds up tension very slowly, only to release it at the very end of the book like a bow string released in one moment. <br /><br />It is a great counterpoint to Dune but read it only if you are invested in this universe. At the same time it is only a segue to Children of Dune which made it possible that SF entered mainstream. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4121917841494815380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_69290372" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69290372" />
    <a title="Lolly&#39;s Library" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2649865-lolly-s-library"><img alt="Lolly&#39;s Library" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503734420p2/2649865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69290372?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lolly&#39;s Library" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lolly&#39;s Library" href="/user/show/2649865-lolly-s-library">Lolly&#39;s Library</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780441015610" href="/book/show/1495434.Dune_Messiah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2649865-lolly-s-library?shelf=part-of-a-series">part-of-a-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2649865-lolly-s-library?shelf=epic-sci-fi">epic-sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69290372" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4651331693420173387">I think most people don't particularly like this book, but I'm not sure why. Is it because Paul-Muad'Dib, Messiah, Emperor, God, is shown as a flawed human? Is it because we see that even with his awesome powers, he's still unable to map the future, to escape the future, the same as any ordinary human? We know Paul was never going to be perfect, was never going to be an angelic being or benevolent emperor; Frank Herbert told us that in "Dune." We know that Paul knew his destiny, knew the consequ</span>
  <span id="freeText4651331693420173387" style="display:none">I think most people don't particularly like this book, but I'm not sure why. Is it because Paul-Muad'Dib, Messiah, Emperor, God, is shown as a flawed human? Is it because we see that even with his awesome powers, he's still unable to map the future, to escape the future, the same as any ordinary human? We know Paul was never going to be perfect, was never going to be an angelic being or benevolent emperor; Frank Herbert told us that in "Dune." We know that Paul knew his destiny, knew the consequences of his actions, from the earliest moments; we can speculate that he might've even had the power to change the outcome, to escape the jihad fought in his name, to fling off the mantle of power that weighed upon him and turned his friends and companions into slavish minions, willing to do anything in the name of Muad'Dib. And yet he didn't. He continued on his course of actions, perhaps because, in his arrogance, he began to believe too much in his own mythology--Muad'Dib, the Kwisatz Haderch, the Lisan al-Gaib; perhaps he even grew to enjoy the trappings of power, underneath his disdain. And perhaps that is what truly destroyed him, in the end: recognition of his human-ness underneath the godhead. I found this book to be just as powerful as "Dune" as it explores what happens to the messiah once he is accepted and the changes he's wrought become routine and ritualized. It wasn't about the world-shaking changes he brought to everyone else; it was about the psyche-shaking changes his role brought to himself, the dark side of power that defines who and what we become. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4651331693420173387" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2232458852" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2232458852?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rose" href="/user/show/7891507-rose">Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780441172696" href="/book/show/106.Dune_Messiah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7891507-rose?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2232458852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1602042978730268169">While it wasn't as grand (or as long) as Dune, I would say Dune Messiah was a very important part of the Dune series. This is the link between Paul and his becoming Muad'dib and his children's rule over Arrakis. Even though it was shorter, and we were already familiar with this dune world, there were many important things that took place. This is a must read for Dune fans. <br/><br/>Now, the reason for the three stars...Dear God I hate Herbert's writing style. Blah, blah, blah, gibber jabber. It felt at t</span>
  <span id="freeText1602042978730268169" style="display:none">While it wasn't as grand (or as long) as Dune, I would say Dune Messiah was a very important part of the Dune series. This is the link between Paul and his becoming Muad'dib and his children's rule over Arrakis. Even though it was shorter, and we were already familiar with this dune world, there were many important things that took place. This is a must read for Dune fans. <br /><br />Now, the reason for the three stars...Dear God I hate Herbert's writing style. Blah, blah, blah, gibber jabber. It felt at times to be a rambling mess. You had to read at a macro level skimming at times just to understand. Paying attention to the details only weighed you down and you had the potential to forget what the characters were even talking about. <br /><br />If you can get past the blathering dialogue, then I highly recommend this to science fiction fans and especially those who read Dune and opted to treat it as a stand-alone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1602042978730268169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19784576-shadowdenizen?shelf=dune">dune</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3634466837" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2646087741430876221">4.5 stars.<br/><br/>After the whirlwind tour-de-force that was "DUNE", I can understand why this book is more polarizing to the general audience, as it focuses far more on the political intrigue aspects, while at the same time making Paul Atreides a more aggressive/less likable character. It's also much more subudued, the "action" not really ramping up until the last quarter of the short novel (and it's roughly half the size of the original "Dune" novel.)<br/><br/>That said, I found this book to be thrilling, a dee</span>
  <span id="freeText2646087741430876221" style="display:none">4.5 stars.<br /><br />After the whirlwind tour-de-force that was "DUNE", I can understand why this book is more polarizing to the general audience, as it focuses far more on the political intrigue aspects, while at the same time making Paul Atreides a more aggressive/less likable character. It's also much more subudued, the "action" not really ramping up until the last quarter of the short novel (and it's roughly half the size of the original "Dune" novel.)<br /><br />That said, I found this book to be thrilling, a deeper look into the mechanics of destiny, and how much one can ACTUALLY control, even as a nigh-omnipotent being. There's also a deeper look into some intriguing elements of the Dune universe (like the Navigators and the BEnne Gesserit).<br /><br />For me, this is highly recommended, but I can understand why large swathes of readers don't generally seem to read beyond the initial novel.<br /><br /><br /></span>

      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2950369455">
  <td colspan="2">
    <div class="updateActionLinks">

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_2950369455'><a id="like_count_review_2950369455" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/2950369455?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">18 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F44492285-dune-messiah">Like</a></span>

  </td>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_18718245" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18718245" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18718245?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780441172696" href="/book/show/106.Dune_Messiah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=imaginary-history">imaginary-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=geekdom">geekdom</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=about-imagination">about-imagination</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=exceeded-my-expectations">exceeded-my-expectations</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18718245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9458831417317229879">More thought experiment mindfuck than Space Opera spectacle (as it's predecessor was), <i>Dune Messiah</i> imagines the whys and wherefores and whatabouts concerning prescience with a power that seems definitive and, in that way, inhibiting to all prescience tales that have followed it and are still to come. <br/><br/>It's the same mechanism as what <i>Back to the Future</i> has done to the way we think about time travel. Generations of audiences -- at least a couple, so far -- believe in inevitable paradoxes within ti</span>
  <span id="freeText9458831417317229879" style="display:none">More thought experiment mindfuck than Space Opera spectacle (as it's predecessor was), <i>Dune Messiah</i> imagines the whys and wherefores and whatabouts concerning prescience with a power that seems definitive and, in that way, inhibiting to all prescience tales that have followed it and are still to come. <br /><br />It's the same mechanism as what <i>Back to the Future</i> has done to the way we think about time travel. Generations of audiences -- at least a couple, so far -- believe in inevitable paradoxes within time travel, that ripples in the time stream will change everything, that people, places and things will disappear under the influence of the smallest interferences. They are sure of it to the point that "new" imaginings of time travel (and even older imaginings of time travel, like <i>The City on the Edge of Forever</i>) are criticized as "breaking the rules" of time travel or "failing to make sense."<br /><br />Of course, time travel is entirely theoretical. Any story that includes time travel is, by its nature, a thought experiment and nothing more. There is no time travel, therefore there can be no right or wrong (so long as it maintains its internal logic) when it comes to time travel (at least not until there <u>is</u> time travel and we can actually start to understand its actual "laws"). <br /><br />The same applies to prescience, and I am sure that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/58.Frank_Herbert" title="Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Frank Herbert</a>'s masterful imagining of what prescience may be must inform the way all those familiar with his work will imagine prescience from the moment they put down <i>Dune Messiah</i> <br /><br />Muad'dib's prescience is so fully imagined that I find it hard to imagine, at least now that I've reread the text after thirty-five+ years, what elements of prescience Herbert might have missed. If some day, some one, some where can foresee the future, I imagine prescience playing out in precisely the way Herbert imagined it would manifest in Paul Atreides (so there is me, fooled by a great thought experiment). <br /><br />Whatever shortcomings this book has (and there are a few), they are eclipsed by the fierce creativity behind Herbert's imaginings of foresight. He does for Sci-Fi prescience in <i>Dune Messiah</i> what he did for Sci-Fi ecology in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune__Dune_Chronicles___1_" title="Dune (Dune Chronicles, #1) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune</a> -- shaping others' narratives for years to come. It's an impressive feat. He may not be my favourite Sci-Fi writer (not even close), but his brilliance has my respect.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9458831417317229879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_82799317" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82799317" />
    <a title="Jeff Yoak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2576733-jeff-yoak"><img alt="Jeff Yoak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1248945780p2/2576733.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82799317?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff Yoak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff Yoak" href="/user/show/2576733-jeff-yoak">Jeff Yoak</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781427202369" href="/book/show/463168.Dune_Messiah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2576733-jeff-yoak?shelf=audio-collection">audio-collection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2576733-jeff-yoak?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2576733-jeff-yoak?shelf=read-in-2001">read-in-2001</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82799317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13176318487593509806">This book was every bit as terrible as I remembered. I was committed to not abandoning it as I did last time because I want to delve a little further into the Dune series. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune__Dune_Chronicles___1_' title='Dune (Dune Chronicles, #1) by Frank Herbert' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dune</a> is one of my favorite novels. Even through there is precedent, it is hard to accept that sequels can be such a complete reversal.<br/><br/>Dune is a strong story about an interesting life. A minor weakness of the book is that it is asserted, but never shown, that the events unfolding will impact inter-galactic empires, create a hol</span>
  <span id="freeText13176318487593509806" style="display:none">This book was every bit as terrible as I remembered. I was committed to not abandoning it as I did last time because I want to delve a little further into the Dune series. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/234225.Dune__Dune_Chronicles___1_" title="Dune (Dune Chronicles, #1) by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Dune</a> is one of my favorite novels. Even through there is precedent, it is hard to accept that sequels can be such a complete reversal.<br /><br />Dune is a strong story about an interesting life. A minor weakness of the book is that it is asserted, but never shown, that the events unfolding will impact inter-galactic empires, create a holy jihad and cause the rise of a major religion centering on the main character. This fails to hurt the book because of none of this actually happens within the confines of Dune, aside from a minor scene at the end that crowns him. This event, in itself, is consistent with the plot.<br /><br />Dune Messiah starts with having accepted that all we were told to expect has happened and then wallows in the religious weirdness it creates. Very little happens. I'm not sure it is possible to recover from here, but will try <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8673762.Children_of_Dune" title="Children of Dune by Frank Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Children of Dune</a> before giving up.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13176318487593509806" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jMEojgpJVLrtrhYEhqzapp84zVT/pJ1r82zPaq3H8gxB4jeObbFNcyBlAwfk8tO35TMUGgxfUfbeEZLUKsB5TQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('M8G4aCqv4C00nfW/+XE1xZl35tCmr71PtPFnxPW/2t3+4qdoTVf55PlW4LybLzzU43w/nlVUcdKZjDp6crhRnA==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/58.Frank_Herbert">About Frank Herbert</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

</a></div>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9669219077268998532">Frank Herbert was a critically acclaimed and commercially successful American science fiction author.<br/><br/>He is best known for the novel <i>Dune</i> and its five sequels. The Dune saga, set in the distant future and taking place over millennia, dealt with themes such as human survival and evolution, ecology, and the intersection of religion, politics, and power, and is widely considered to be among the classi</span>
  <span id="freeText9669219077268998532" style="display:none">Frank Herbert was a critically acclaimed and commercially successful American science fiction author.<br /><br />He is best known for the novel <i>Dune</i> and its five sequels. The Dune saga, set in the distant future and taking place over millennia, dealt with themes such as human survival and evolution, ecology, and the intersection of religion, politics, and power, and is widely considered to be among the classics in the field of science fiction.<br /><br />He was the father of fellow author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/56.Brian_Herbert" title="Brian Herbert" rel="nofollow noopener">Brian Herbert</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9669219077268998532" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
